<meta name="DC.Title" content="Plant evolution and environmental adaptation unveiled by long-read whole-genome sequencing of Spirodela" />

<meta name="DC.Description" content="Constant exposure of aquatic plants to freely exchangeable nutrients and pathogenic microbes requires regulation of gene expression different to land plants. However, short-read sequencing platforms fail to provide vital information that comprises genes involved in the response to such a challenge. Here, we applied long-read sequencing to retrieve missing sequences to the duckweed species of Spirodela polyrhiza . Evolution of the genetic network and root morphology show that roots play a function as sea anchors rather than nutrient uptake. Moreover, disease-resistance gene clusters are constitutively active whereas they are silenced by phasiRNA in land plants." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="Plant evolution and environmental adaptation unveiled by long-read whole-genome sequencing of Spirodela" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Aquatic plants have to adapt to the environments distinct from where land plants grow. A critical aspect of adaptation is the dynamics of sequence repeats, not resolved in older sequencing platforms due to incomplete and fragmented genome assemblies from short reads. Therefore, we used PacBio long-read sequencing of the &lt;i&gt;Spirodela polyrhiza&lt;/i&gt; genome, reaching a 44-fold increase of contiguity with an N50 (a median of contig lengths) of 831 kb and filling 95.4% of gaps left from the previous version. Reconstruction of repeat regions indicates that sequentially nested long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotranspositions occur early in monocot evolution, featured with both prokaryote-like gene-rich regions and eukaryotic repeat islands. Protein-coding genes are reduced to 18,708 gene models supported by 492,435 high-quality full-length PacBio complementary DNA (cDNA) sequences. Different from land plants, the primitive architecture of &lt;i&gt;Spirodela&lt;/i&gt;’s adventitious roots and lack of lateral roots and root hairs are consistent with dispensable functions of nutrient absorption. Disease-resistant genes encoding antimicrobial peptides and dirigent proteins are expanded by tandem duplications. Remarkably, disease-resistant genes are not only amplified, but also highly expressed, consistent with low levels of 24-nucleotide (nt) small interfering RNA (siRNA) that silence the immune system of land plants, thereby protecting &lt;i&gt;Spirodela&lt;/i&gt; against a wide spectrum of pathogens and pests. The long-read sequence information not only sheds light on plant evolution and adaptation to the environment, but also facilitates applications in bioenergy and phytoremediation.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Constant exposure of aquatic plants to freely exchangeable nutrients and pathogenic microbes requires regulation of gene expression different to land plants. However, short-read sequencing platforms fail to provide vital information that comprises genes involved in the response to such a challenge. Here, we applied long-read sequencing to retrieve missing sequences to the duckweed species of &lt;i&gt;Spirodela polyrhiza&lt;/i&gt;. Evolution of the genetic network and root morphology show that roots play a function as sea anchors rather than nutrient uptake. Moreover, disease-resistance gene clusters are constitutively active whereas they are silenced by phasiRNA in land plants.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Plant evolution and environmental adaptation unveiled by long-read whole-genome sequencing of Spirodela" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/highwire/pnas/116/38.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Constant exposure of aquatic plants to freely exchangeable nutrients and pathogenic microbes requires regulation of gene expression different to land plants. However, short-read sequencing platforms fail to provide vital information that comprises genes involved in the response to such a challenge. Here, we applied long-read sequencing to retrieve missing sequences to the duckweed species of Spirodela polyrhiza . Evolution of the genetic network and root morphology show that roots play a function as sea anchors rather than nutrient uptake. Moreover, disease-resistance gene clusters are constitutively active whereas they are silenced by phasiRNA in land plants." />
<meta name="og-title" property="og:title" content="Plant evolution and environmental adaptation unveiled by long-read whole-genome sequencing of Spirodela" />
<meta name="og-url" property="og:url" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/116/38/18893" />
<meta name="og-site-name" property="og:site_name" content="PNAS" />
<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Constant exposure of aquatic plants to freely exchangeable nutrients and pathogenic microbes requires regulation of gene expression different to land plants. However, short-read sequencing platforms fail to provide vital information that comprises genes involved in the response to such a challenge. Here, we applied long-read sequencing to retrieve missing sequences to the duckweed species of Spirodela polyrhiza . Evolution of the genetic network and root morphology show that roots play a function as sea anchors rather than nutrient uptake. Moreover, disease-resistance gene clusters are constitutively active whereas they are silenced by phasiRNA in land plants." />

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="888631" id="node888631" data-pisa="pnas;116/38/18893" data-pisa-master="pnas;1910401116" data-apath="/pnas/116/38/18893.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-pnas-article-title-complete clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">
  
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-overline"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-article-category highwire-cite-metadata">Research Article</span></div>
  
  
      <h1 class="highwire-cite-title" id="page-title">Plant evolution and environmental adaptation unveiled by long-read whole-genome sequencing of <em>Spirodela</em></h1>  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-authors"><span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Dong An</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="1"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-1662-9589" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Yong Zhou</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="2">Changsheng Li</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="3">Qiao Xiao</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="4">Tao Wang</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="5">Yating Zhang</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="6"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3822-0511" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Yongrui Wu</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="7">Yubin Li</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="8">Dai-Yin Chao</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="9"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8260-2643" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Joachim Messing</span>, and <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="10">Wenqin Wang</span></span></div>
  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-metadata"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-journal highwire-cite-metadata">PNAS </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-date highwire-cite-metadata">September 17, 2019 </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-volume highwire-cite-metadata">116 </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-issue highwire-cite-metadata">(38) </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-pages highwire-cite-metadata">18893-18899; </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-papdate highwire-cite-metadata">first published September 4, 2019; </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-doi highwire-cite-metadata"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1910401116">https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1910401116</a> </span></div>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-6">Constant exposure of aquatic plants to freely exchangeable nutrients and pathogenic microbes requires regulation of gene expression different to land plants. However, short-read sequencing platforms fail to provide vital information that comprises genes involved in the response to such a challenge. Here, we applied long-read sequencing to retrieve missing sequences to the duckweed species of <em>Spirodela polyrhiza</em>. Evolution of the genetic network and root morphology show that roots play a function as sea anchors rather than nutrient uptake. Moreover, disease-resistance gene clusters are constitutively active whereas they are silenced by phasiRNA in land plants.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-7">Aquatic plants have to adapt to the environments distinct from where land plants grow. A critical aspect of adaptation is the dynamics of sequence repeats, not resolved in older sequencing platforms due to incomplete and fragmented genome assemblies from short reads. Therefore, we used PacBio long-read sequencing of the <em>Spirodela polyrhiza</em> genome, reaching a 44-fold increase of contiguity with an N50 (a median of contig lengths) of 831 kb and filling 95.4% of gaps left from the previous version. Reconstruction of repeat regions indicates that sequentially nested long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotranspositions occur early in monocot evolution, featured with both prokaryote-like gene-rich regions and eukaryotic repeat islands. Protein-coding genes are reduced to 18,708 gene models supported by 492,435 high-quality full-length PacBio complementary DNA (cDNA) sequences. Different from land plants, the primitive architecture of <em>Spirodela</em>’s adventitious roots and lack of lateral roots and root hairs are consistent with dispensable functions of nutrient absorption. Disease-resistant genes encoding antimicrobial peptides and dirigent proteins are expanded by tandem duplications. Remarkably, disease-resistant genes are not only amplified, but also highly expressed, consistent with low levels of 24-nucleotide (nt) small interfering RNA (siRNA) that silence the immune system of land plants, thereby protecting <em>Spirodela</em> against a wide spectrum of pathogens and pests. The long-read sequence information not only sheds light on plant evolution and adaptation to the environment, but also facilitates applications in bioenergy and phytoremediation.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/long-reads" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-long-reads" rel="nofollow">long reads</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/root-evolution" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-root-evolution" rel="nofollow">root evolution</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/disease-resistance" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-disease-resistance" rel="nofollow">disease resistance</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/tandem-duplication" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-tandem-duplication" rel="nofollow">tandem duplication</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/aquatic-adaptation" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-aquatic-adaptation" rel="nofollow">aquatic adaptation</a></li></ul><p id="p-8" class="flushleft">Greater duckweeds, i.e., <em>Spirodela polyrhiza</em>, are small and fast-growing aquatic plants, belonging to the early-diverging monocot order of the Alismatales (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>) (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1</a>). As one of the most yielding biomass species, they can be found from temperate to tropical regions, surviving in freshwater ponds and animal waste lagoons. The plant is composed of a tiny frond and a few adventitious roots (ARs), providing unique material to study root evolution at early angiosperm lineages. Two other genera of <em>Lemnaceae</em> (<em>Wolffiella</em> and <em>Wolffia</em>) appear to lack roots, based on close morphological and microscopic inspections (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). Because of larger fronds of the genus <em>Spirodela</em>, sticky roots perhaps help maintain their upright position in the water and promote vegetative dispersal with the attachment to animals, rather than act primarily as an organ for the uptake of water and nutrients (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). On the other hand, the presence of water brings the fronds into immediate contact with a diverse population of microbes. Interestingly, the duckweed crude extract exhibited antimicrobial resistance toward waterborne fungi and bacteria (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>, <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>), which could be a potential source for medicinal herbs and new antimicrobial therapeutics. To understand the molecular nature of these properties, a comprehensive repertoire of genes is essential, not available with older sequencing platforms. Due to larger gaps from short-read (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>) sequence assembly, missing information could be critical for understanding the molecular mechanism of root formation and defenses against diseases. The previous assembly of the <em>Spirodela</em> genome was based on a read length of ∼350 base pairs (bp) (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>), too short to assess critical properties of plant aquatic adaptation. Indeed, the assembly included more than 16,055 small contigs and 11.8% of missing sequences, limiting access to total gene content. Here, we were able to extend read length to more than 10 kilobases (kb), using PacBio long-read sequencing. We filled 95.4% of preexisting gaps, and, with PacBio sequencing of full-length complementary DNA (cDNA), we could identify 18,708 protein-coding genes, many of which were larger than previously thought. In particular, the expansion of disease-resistant gene families by tandem duplications can explain the innate immunity and worldwide distribution of the species. Furthermore, new sequence information gives us new insights into the minimal morphological requirements of a higher plant and its environmental adaptation.</p><div class="section results-discussion" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results and Discussion</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing, Assembly, and Annotation.</h3><p id="p-9">A clone of <em>S. polyrhiza</em> 7498 from North Carolina, United States, had been subjected to physical mapping and whole-genome shotgun sequencing using the “454” platform (<a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). Because of the short reads (∼350 bp) of the “454” system, the genome of <em>Spirodela</em> (Sp7498V2) was highly fragmented and could not resolve complex regions, leading to genome misassembly or misannotations. Although shotgun DNA sequencing (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>) facilitated the assembly of a complete genome (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>), the presence of tandemly repeated sequences, like gene copies, transposable elements, or satellite repeats of large genomes, prevents earlier developed sequencing platforms from delivering contiguous sequence information. PacBio with long reads (&gt;10 kb) can cover sequence junctions between single repeats, such as retrotransposons, and can overcome assembly problems in terms of gap filling and repeat reconstruction in many plants, such as maize (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>, <a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>), Oropetium (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>), quinoa (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>), and broomcorn millet (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>). To this end, we resequenced <em>S. polyrhiza</em> 7498 with ∼126-fold coverage of PacBio long reads, resulting in 411 contigs and an N50 length (a median of contig lengths) of 831 kb (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>) (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S2 and S3 and Tables S1 and S2</a>), which improved contiguous sequence information 44 times compared with the previous version (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-7-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). The new genome assembly version, named Sp7498V3, presents the highest contiguity and the fewest gaps compared to another ecotype, <em>S. polyrhiza</em> 9509 (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>), and to <em>Lemna minor</em> (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>) assembled from Illumina short reads. The older version, Sp7498V2, had 13,459 gaps, with 11.8% unknown sequences, whereas Sp7498V3 had only 270 gaps, leaving only 4.6% of missing sequences (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4 and Table S3</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/888795/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/888795/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <p id="p-10" class="first-child">Comparison of genome assembly of <em>Spirodela</em> from short reads and long reads</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/38/18893/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparison of genome assembly from short reads and long reads. </ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-12" class="first-child">Comparison of genome assembly from short reads and long reads. From outside to inside, the circles represent karyotype (a), sequence gaps (b), GC content (c), full-length LTRs (d), gene density (e), and syntenous connections (f). The metrics are calculated in 1-Mb sliding windows. The right half circle represents genome assembly from long reads (Sp7498V3). The left half circle represents genome assembly from short reads (Sp7498V2). Every blue vertical bar indicates one gap in layer b. There are 270 gaps in Sp7498V3 and 13,459 gaps in Sp7498V2. The inner lines denote the synteny of two versions of genomes. Chr, chromosome.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-13">Combining bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) end sequences (BESs), 411 contigs were placed into 227 scaffolds with an N50 of 3.3 Mb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>), merged into 20 chromosomes with the aid of FISH (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>). Still, there were certain contigs equal to 7.1 Mb unassigned to any chromosome. The quality and integrity of chromosomes were confirmed with the physical map and 20 fully sequenced 40-kb fosmids (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-7-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). The fosmids matched the <em>Spirodela</em> genome assembly (Sp7498V3) with more than 98.6% sequence identity, all within a single contig from the long-read assembly (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S4</a>).</p><p id="p-14">A total of 545,753 full-length transcript sequences were generated by PacBio isoform sequencing (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>) (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S5</a>), which resulted in 492,435 high-quality consensus sequences. The annotation of the <em>Spirodela</em> genome was improved in combination with de novo prediction and cDNA data, leading to a level of 74.6% of 18,708 protein-coding genes supported by full-length transcripts. The number for predicted gene models is slightly lower (4.66%) than those from Sp7498V2 (19,623), mainly due to the merger of gene fragments. For example, the genes of Spo013477, Spo003046, and Spo000617 in Sp7498V3 were concatenated from 4, 3, and 2 genes in Sp7498V2, respectively (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5</a>). The average gene length was significantly increased by 24.5% from 3,458 to 4,342 bp, with longer and higher exon numbers for each gene (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-3" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) evaluation revealed that Sp7498V3 had a higher rate (86%) of complete homologous proteins, compared with 79% of Sp7498V2 (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a>), indicating a high-quality assembly and annotation of Sp7498V3.</p><p id="p-15">Repetitive elements play important roles in shaping genome architecture and gene regulation. Most repeats were incomplete, unassembled, or highly collapsed in Sp7498V2 due to short-read assembly. The improved genome of Sp7498V3 allowed us to accurately survey its completeness of repetitive features. We found that repetitive sequences account for 30.91% of the genome (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6 and Table S7</a>), 18.61% of which were long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons that arose before 2 million years ago (MYA) and were species-specific (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S7 and Table S8</a>). A total of 156 retrotranspositions occurred on top of each other (nested versions) whereas 1,544 intact LTR retrotransposons were present in Sp7498V3, which was comparable to that of only 722 in Sp7498V2. A close view of scaffold 15 illustrated that genes and transposable elements preferentially were located in individual islands, resembling both prokaryotic and eukaryotic features in <em>Spirodela</em> chromosomes (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/38/18893/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="A benchmark of the nested LTRs in Spirodela. Scaffold 15 with contiguous sequence length of 539 kb exhibits regions of TE islands and a gene cluster. Zone I and IV are nested with LTR regions. Red numbers next to LTRs represent insertion ages in million years ago (MYA). Retrotransposons are indicated by filled triangles of different colors. Zone II and Zone V are simple repeat regions. Simple repeats are represented by blue vertical lines. Zone III contains 23 genes without any transposon interruption. The genes are shown as green pentagons or triangles. Genes and LTRs have been drawn to scale. Hap, Doc, Sne, Sle, Dop, and Bas indicate different types of LTR retrotransposons." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-433693378" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;A benchmark of the nested LTRs in &lt;em&gt;Spirodela&lt;/em&gt;. </ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-16" class="first-child">A benchmark of the nested LTRs in <em>Spirodela</em>. Scaffold 15 with contiguous sequence length of 539 kb exhibits regions of TE islands and a gene cluster. Zone I and IV are nested with LTR regions. Red numbers next to LTRs represent insertion ages in million years ago (MYA). Retrotransposons are indicated by filled triangles of different colors. Zone II and Zone V are simple repeat regions. Simple repeats are represented by blue vertical lines. Zone III contains 23 genes without any transposon interruption. The genes are shown as green pentagons or triangles. Genes and LTRs have been drawn to scale. Hap, Doc, Sne, Sle, Dop, and Bas indicate different types of LTR retrotransposons.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Comparative Genomics.</h3><p id="p-17">The identification of syntenic regions between the <em>Spirodela</em> genome itself revealed two rounds of whole genome duplications (WGDs), as expected (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S8</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-7-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). The dot plot map showed that more syntenic segmental blocks were retrieved in Sp7498V3 than Sp7498V2 (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S8</a>), indicating that the improved genome assembly uncovered more intragenomic collinearity. Conserved single copy protein sequences were aligned to form a divergence tree for 9 species [<em>Zostera marina</em> (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>), <em>Phoenix dactylifera</em> (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>), <em>Ananas comosus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>), <em>Oryza sativa</em> (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>), <em>Sorghum bicolor</em> (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>), <em>Zea mays</em> (<a id="xref-ref-10-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>), <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>), <em>Solanum lycopersicum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>), and <em>S. polyrhiza</em>]. <em>Spirodela</em> and <em>Zostera</em> were clustered together as sister clades compared with all other monocots. They diverged between 118.5 and 129.0 million years ago (MYA). <em>Spirodela</em> and other terrestrial monocots were separated somewhere between 130.4 and 140.4 MYA (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S9</a>). The tree could help us to understand how plants adapt to different living media, including sea zone, freshwater area, and terrestrial land with a spectrum of sequenced genomes.</p><p id="p-18">One answer to such a question comes from the comparison of gene families of <em>Spirodela</em>, <em>Zostera</em>, <em>Zea</em>, <em>Oryza</em>, and <em>Arabidopsis</em>. There were 7,647 gene families shared by all 5 species whereas 674 families were specifically lost in <em>Zostera</em> and present in the other 4 species (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S10</a>). Gene ontology (GO) analysis showed significant enrichment for positive regulation of stomatal complex development, response to ultraviolet (UV), starch catabolic process, regulation of DNA repair, glyoxylate cycle, tryptophan biosynthetic process, gamma-tubulin complex localization, and regulation of proteasomal ubiquitin-dependent protein catabolic process. The lost functions were consistent with that <em>Zostera</em> need not to cope with intense UV radiation and not to exchange gas with stomata under marine conditions (<a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>). In contrast, there were 420 gene families that were lost in <em>Spirodela</em> and <em>Zostera,</em> in comparison with rice, maize, and <em>Arabidopsis</em>. GO enrichment analysis indicated that the biosynthetic and metabolic processes of secondary metabolites, including sesquiterpene, terpene, terpenoid, and isoprenoid, were overrepresented in these species. These secondary metabolites are a large and diverse class of naturally occurring organic chemicals, playing a key role as precursors to overcome gravity and dry land (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>). We hypothesize that <em>Spirodela</em> and <em>Zostera</em> do not require complex metabolites to cope with variable temperature, light, and nutrients under relatively stable aquatic environments.</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Evolution of Root Development.</h3><p id="p-19">A major difference between land and aquatic plants is the intake pathway of water and nutrients. The <em>Spirodela</em> sticky root system appears to be more critical for securing an anchoring position and promoting a wide distribution rather than absorbing nutrients. It was reported that the lower surface of the frond served as the main organ for nutrient uptake (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). The attachment of roots to animals and birds allows duckweeds to be transported to a distant location, thus aiding geographic dispersal without human intervention (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). When the lower surface was painted with waterproof lanolin, duckweeds grew more slowly than control plants (<a href="http://www.missouribotanicalgarden.org/" class="in-nw">http://www.missouribotanicalgarden.org</a>). As we cut the roots from mother fronds, <em>Spirodela</em> continued to grow by producing daughter fronds after 3 d, keeping pace with the control plants bearing intact roots (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S11</a>). <em>Spirodela</em> has as many as 12 adventitious roots (ARs) per frond but lacks secondary lateral roots (LRs) and root hairs (RHs) (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>). In addition, the genera of <em>Wolffiella</em> and <em>Wolffia</em> do not even have any roots (<a id="xref-ref-2-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). ARs, usually derived from shoots, stems, or leaves, are prevalent in monocots, which is different from eudicots containing lateral roots (LRs) (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). We examined the root structures and observed epidermis, cortex, and vascular tissue, but no root hairs (RHs) in the cross-section of ARs (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3 <em>C</em>–<em>F</em></a>). The epidermis is the outermost boundary. The cortex is loosely packed in parenchymatous cells with large intercellular air spaces, allowing gaseous exchange and providing buoyancy. The vascular tissue is highly primitive in <em>Spirodela</em>, with a tracheary element in the middle surrounded by a ring of phloem tissue (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3 <em>C</em>–<em>F</em></a>). The simple architecture is consistent with its function of maintaining the stability of the plant body.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/38/18893/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Anatomy of the Spirodela root. (A). A dorsal overview of a Spirodela plant, showing the location of cross-sections. (Scale bar: 500 &#x3BC;m.) The cross-sections were sampled at 8 (c), 1.5 (d), 0.5 (e), and 0.2 mm (f) from the root tip, corresponding to Fig. 3 C&#x2013;F, respectively. (B). A ventral overview of a Spirodela plant exhibits as many as 12 adventitious roots. (Scale bar: 500 &#x3BC;m.) (C&#x2013;F) Cross-sections of Spirodela roots, illustrating the structures of the epidermis (Ep), cortex (C), endodermis (En), and vascular tissue (V). A close investigation shows vascular tissues. The central cell (X), located in the middle, is a tracheary element, which is surrounded by a ring of phloem tissue (P). The endodermis (En) is between the vascular tissue and the cortex parenchyma cells (Pc). (Scale bars: 50 &#x3BC;m.) (G) The genetic pathways involved in adventitious root (AR), lateral root (LR), and root hair (RH) development. The homologous genes in Spirodela are defined by using the rice protein sequences." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-433693378" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Anatomy of the &lt;em&gt;Spirodela&lt;/em&gt; root. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;). A dorsal overview of a &lt;em&gt;Spirodela&lt;/em&gt; plant, showing the location of cross-sections. (Scale bar: 500 &#x3BC;m.) The cross-sections were sampled at 8 (c), 1.5 (d), 0.5 (e), and 0.2 mm (f) from the root tip, corresponding to Fig. 3 &lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;&#x2013;&lt;em&gt;F&lt;/em&gt;, respectively. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;). A ventral overview of a &lt;em&gt;Spirodela&lt;/em&gt; plant exhibits as many as 12 adventitious roots. (Scale bar: 500 &#x3BC;m.) (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;&#x2013;&lt;em&gt;F&lt;/em&gt;) Cross-sections of &lt;em&gt;Spirodela&lt;/em&gt; roots, illustrating the structures of the epidermis (Ep), cortex (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;), endodermis (En), and vascular tissue (V). A close investigation shows vascular tissues. The central cell (X), located in the middle, is a tracheary element, which is surrounded by a ring of phloem tissue (P). The endodermis (En) is between the vascular tissue and the cortex parenchyma cells (Pc). (Scale bars: 50 &#x3BC;m.) (&lt;em&gt;G&lt;/em&gt;) The genetic pathways involved in adventitious root (AR), lateral root (LR), and root hair (RH) development. The homologous genes in &lt;em&gt;Spirodela&lt;/em&gt; are defined by using the rice protein sequences.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/38/18893/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="422"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/38/18893/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="422"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/38/18893/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/38/18893/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/888756" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-20" class="first-child">Anatomy of the <em>Spirodela</em> root. (<em>A</em>). A dorsal overview of a <em>Spirodela</em> plant, showing the location of cross-sections. (Scale bar: 500 μm.) The cross-sections were sampled at 8 (c), 1.5 (d), 0.5 (e), and 0.2 mm (f) from the root tip, corresponding to Fig. 3 <em>C</em>–<em>F</em>, respectively. (<em>B</em>). A ventral overview of a <em>Spirodela</em> plant exhibits as many as 12 adventitious roots. (Scale bar: 500 μm.) (<em>C</em>–<em>F</em>) Cross-sections of <em>Spirodela</em> roots, illustrating the structures of the epidermis (Ep), cortex (<em>C</em>), endodermis (En), and vascular tissue (V). A close investigation shows vascular tissues. The central cell (X), located in the middle, is a tracheary element, which is surrounded by a ring of phloem tissue (P). The endodermis (En) is between the vascular tissue and the cortex parenchyma cells (Pc). (Scale bars: 50 μm.) (<em>G</em>) The genetic pathways involved in adventitious root (AR), lateral root (LR), and root hair (RH) development. The homologous genes in <em>Spirodela</em> are defined by using the rice protein sequences.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-21">The layers of pith, conjunctive tissue, xylem (protoxylem and metaxylem), and phloem (protophloem and metaphloem) have been investigated in roots of rice, which are required to provide mechanical support and to improve water and nutrient transportation over a short or long distance (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>). Therefore, we used rice protein sequences involved in AR root development to search for homologous genes in <em>Spirodela</em>. <em>Spirodela</em> shared all of the genes with rice for AR’s initiation and elongation (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>G</em></a>), supporting a conserved mechanism of AR evolution as early as in early-diverging monocots. But, comparably, <em>Spirodela</em> had lost gene members associated with LR initiation (ZFP, NAL2, and NAL3), as well as with LR elongation (ORC3 and SLL1). Genes responsible for RH development (RSL, WOX3A, SNDP, and RHL1) were also absent, resulting in an incomplete RH pathway. The loss of lateral roots and root hairs is consistent with the reduced functions of nutrient uptake in duckweeds but would not significantly affect their function as a sea anchor, as well as helping vegetative dispersal.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Disease-Resistant Genes in Tandem Duplications.</h3><p id="p-22">Whole-genome shotgun DNA sequencing based on long reads is critical for assemblies of tandemly repeated gene copies (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>). In <em>Spirodela</em>, this became evident because disease-resistant genes are mostly enriched in tandem duplications, including gene families encoding antimicrobial peptides and dirigent proteins (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S12</a>). Plant antimicrobial peptides (AMPs) are small defense proteins that constitute a first-line protection against pathogens (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). Compared with 46 antimicrobial peptides (AMPs) in maize (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>), a total of 108 AMP members were found in <em>Spirodela</em>, which included many families, such as defensin, snaking, lipid transfer protein, hevein, and cyclotide, among which the cyclotide family was the most dominant (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S13</a>). It is worth noting that 92 out of 108 AMP genes were tandemly clustered with up to 22 copies in <em>Spirodela</em> (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>A</em></a>), compared to the array of two copies at most in maize (<a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). Dirigent proteins are also reported to be involved in defense response against fungi and insects (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>). They were also induced by abiotic stress, such as drought, low temperature, and abscisic acid (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>). There were 23 gene copies with the largest cluster of 12 tandem duplications in the assembled genome (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1910401116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S12</a>). The tandemly duplicated genes generally maintain a similar function due to their adjacent location and the sharing of the same regulatory elements (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>), which is different from dispersed gene copies that tend to evolve novel functions. Thus, gene amplification affects gene dosage and correlates with a higher level of gene expression (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>). Here, 62 out of 92 AMP genes were supported by full-length cDNA sequences (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>A</em></a>), and 36 ones were expressed higher than the medium level (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>B</em></a>), compared with all expressed genes from RNA-Seq analysis (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>), indicating their constitutive expression to maintaining their active pathogen-resistant ability. Different from land plants, where expression of disease resistance genes is inducible by exposure to pathogens, aquatic plants are in constant contact with a diverse population of microorganisms. This adaptation is also consistent with the low level of the 24-nt siRNAs, possibly due to reduced expression of DCL3 and PolIV in <em>Spirodela</em> fronds (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>), which are known to guide DNA methylation and be involved in the silencing of repeat sequences like retrotransposons and tandemly repeated disease-resistant genes in angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>, <a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>). Because of the neotenous growth of <em>Spirodela</em>, there is less of a need to guard against retrotransposition during meiosis than in land plants, but, as we can see here, also for the repression of its immune system.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/38/18893/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Genomic distribution and transcriptomic expression of tandemly duplicated disease-resistant genes. The sign of * next to the yellow arrowhead indicates that the gene is supported by full-length cDNA evidence. Contig 44 (Ctg44) is one of contigs that could not be incorporated into chromosomes with current sequence data. (B) Gene expression level is represented in the y axis with the value of fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads (FPKM) analyzed from RNA-Seq. The horizontal dotted line shows the average gene expression of total expressed genes. The bars are labeled in different colors based on chromosomes." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-433693378" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Genomic distribution and transcriptomic expression of tandemly duplicated disease-resistant genes. The bars are labeled in different colors based on chromosomes.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="T2" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/888768/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/888768/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 2.</span> <p id="p-24" class="first-child">Gene expression in the pathway of RNA-directed DNA methylation (RdDM)</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-27">Our study illustrates how sequencing with long-read technology can resolve complex repeat regions and gene loci with tandem duplications. Such regions contain critical genomic information that is relevant for the evolution of species and their adaptation to their growth environment, thus helping improve crops through genomic breeding approaches (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>). Structural and physiological adaptations to fresh waters learned from this research effort might accelerate potential applications of duckweed in bioreactors, bioremediation, and biofuel.</p></div></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-6"><h2 class="">Methods</h2><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Whole-Genome Shotgun PacBio Sequencing.</h3><p id="p-28"><em>S. polyrhiza</em> 7498 was grown in SH medium under 16 h light/8 h dark at 28 °C for 7 d. More than 50 μg of genomic DNA was prepared from <em>S. polyrhiza</em> 7498. The SMRTbell library with 20-kb insert was constructed with BluePippin size selection (Sage Science). The resulting SMRTbell templates were sequenced with four SMRT Cells of the PacBio Sequel platform (Pacific Biosciences, Frasergen, Wuhan, China).</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>PacBio Isoform Sequencing.</h3><p id="p-29"><em>S. polyrhiza</em> 7498 was treated by multiple conditions, as follows: 37 °C, 0 °C, desiccation, pH value of 9, UV exposure, 20 mg/L CuCl<sub>2</sub>, 300 mg/L KNO<sub>3</sub>, 250 nM ABA, 10 mM kinetin, and 300 mM mannitol. Total RNA was isolated using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen) and purified with the RNeasy Mini kit after DNase I digestion (Qiagen). RNA was pooled with identical quantities and then subjected to PacBio isoform sequencing (Iso-Seq) using a Clontech SMARTer PCR cDNA synthesis Kit (Clontech). cDNA was size-selected for library construction of 1 to 2 kb, 2 to 3 kb, 3 to 6 kb, and 5 to 10 kb. The templates were sequenced by using P6 polymerase and C4 chemistry. The downstream Iso-Seq analysis was processed by using SMRT-Analysis software v2.3.0 (Pacific Biosciences), including full-length cDNA identification, isoform-level clustering, and final consensus corrections.</p></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>De Novo Genome Assembly.</h3><p id="p-30">The PacBio raw reads were corrected to Preads by Falcon, and then Preads longer than 8 kb were assembled to contigs by using two assemblers, Falcon (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>) and Canu (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>), with optimized parameters. The short Preads smaller than 8 kb were applied to fill gaps. The scaffolds were created by mapping BAC-end sequencing (BES) data to the contigs. The scaffolds were ordered into 20-chromosome-level pseudomolecules after the integration of the last <em>Spirodela</em> genome version (Sp7498V2) (<a id="xref-ref-7-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>), resulting in the final version of Sp7498V3.</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Transposable Elements Prediction.</h3><p id="p-31">De novo repeat identification was conducted by Repeat Modeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/" class="in-nw">http://www.RepeatMasker.org</a>) and Repeat Masker (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>). Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were predicted by LTRdigest (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>) and LTRharvest (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>). Helitrons were predicted by default parameters in HelitronScanner (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>). Short interspersed nuclear elements (SINEs) were defined by SINE-finder (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>). Terminal inverted repeats were predicted from the pipeline TARGeT (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>). The LTR integration time was estimated based on the sequence similarity of 3′ LTR and 5′ LTR aligned with MAFFT (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>). Kimura parameter distances (K) between 5′ LTR and 3′ LTR repeats of each LTR element were calculated with the EMBOSS program (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). The divergence time (T) of intact LTR retrotransposons was calculated using the formula T = K/2r (r is the neutral substitution rate of 1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup> substitutions per site per million years) (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Annotation.</h3><p id="p-32">The structural annotation for protein-coding genes was based on de novo prediction, homologous alignment, and full-length cDNA sequences from PacBio isoform sequencing. The programs of Augustus and GlimmerHMM were utilized to ab initio predict gene loci and structures on the repeat-masked genome, with parameters trained from a set of high-quality proteins and full-length cDNAs, which were manually curated. The protein sets were collected and chosen as homology-based evidence from species of: <em>Z. marina</em> (ORCAE v. 2.1) (<a id="xref-ref-20-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>), <em>O. sativa</em> (Phytozome v. 9.0) (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>), <em>Z. mays</em> (Phytozome v. 9.0) (<a id="xref-ref-10-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>), and <em>A. thaliana</em> (TAIR10) (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>). A number of 492,435 high-quality full-length cDNAs generated by isoform sequencing from <em>Spirodela</em> multiple tissues were used as transcript-based evidence, as well as transcripts assembled from RNA-Seq reads. The pipeline of MAKER was used to combine all evidence to generate nonredundant gene models (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). The functional annotation was assigned by using a sequence homology search. The derived Gene Ontology terms and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) pathway were assigned with the alignment against the databases of InterPro, GO, KEGG, Swiss-Prot, TrEMBL, and NR (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>). The completeness of genome assembly and annotation was assessed by using the analysis of Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO). The syntenic dot plot was generated by using the CoGe platform to perform comparative genomics. The web-based tool of SynMap was used to compare the <em>Spirodela</em> genome to itself to identify syntenic regions with the default blast parameters and an e-value of 0.001 (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>). Following an “all-versus-all” BlastP alignment for the selected species with the e-value of 1e−5, identity of 70%, and coverage of 50%, OrthoMCL was used to identify orthologous groups between and within species applying a Markov Cluster algorithm (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">58</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic Tree.</h3><p id="p-33">All single-copy orthologs with a minimal length of 100 amino acids across the selected genomes were extracted and aligned using MAFFT (v7.221). The alignment was transformed into a supergene alignment with phy format by a custom perl script. A phylogenetic tree was constructed using RAxML (v8.0.26) Maximum Likelihood analysis with a high bootstrap of 100. The divergence time was estimated by r8s based on the topology of the phylogenic tree with the combination of known divergence time (<a href="http://www.timetree.org/" class="in-nw">http://www.timetree.org</a>). To further study gene family expansion or contraction, the sizes of all gene families (excluding orphans and species-specific families) were calculated using CAFÉ (v4.2).</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Tandem Duplicated Genes and Homologous Gene Search.</h3><p id="p-34">Tandem duplicated genes were detected by MCscanX with the similarity search of BlastP (e-value &lt; 1e−10) (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>). The dedicated searches of homologous genes involved in the initiation and elongation of adventitious roots, lateral roots, and root hairs were performed. Putative orthologs were defined using BlastP, with the query chosen from documented pathways in <em>Oryza</em> (<a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>, <a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">60</a>) against the database of <em>Spirodela</em> proteins.</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Histological Analysis of <em>Spirodela</em> Roots.</h3><p id="p-35">Whole roots were fixed with acetic acid:ethanol (1:3 vol:vol) for 24 h, followed with a serial ethanol dehydration (70, 80, 90, 95, and 100%) at room temperature for 0.5 to 1 h at each step. Samples were embedded in Technovit 7100 resin and cut at a thickness of 8 µm. The slides were dried at 65 °C and observed under a microscope (Nikon).</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Data Availability.</h3><p id="p-36">Genome assembly and consensus sequences were deposited into NCBI GenBank with an accession ID of SWLF00000000. Full-length cDNA sequences were derived by error correction of multiple reads, resulting in 492,435 high-quality isoforms that were uploaded with the ID of SRX5321175. RNA-Seq was deposited at GenBank BioProject of PRJNA557001.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-37">We thank Zehua Liu, Lu Wang, Shijun Xiao, and Xiaofei Zeng (Frasergen) for bioinformatic analysis. The project was supported by National Natural Science Foundation of China Grant 31670366 (to W.W.) and the Waksman Chair in Molecular Genetics (J.M.).</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="fn-equal" id="fn-4"><p id="p-4"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-4-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>D.A. and Y.     <a href="http://blog.pnas.org/2021/01/study-reveals-evolutionary-origins-of-fold-switching-protein/" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="field field-name-field-featured-item-image field-type-image field-label-hidden"><div class="field-items"><div class="field-item even"><img src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/styles/medium/public/additional-assets/featured-item/jcb-220.png?itok=gSEyfdzb" width="220" height="220" alt="One red and one yellow modeled protein structures" /></div></div></div></a>  </div> <!-- End of image -->
  
  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="http://blog.pnas.org/2021/01/study-reveals-evolutionary-origins-of-fold-switching-protein/" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Journal Club: Study reveals evolutionary origins of fold-switching protein</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description">Shapeshifting designs could have wide-ranging pharmaceutical and biomedical applications in coming years.</div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__credits text-small">Image credit: Acacia Dishman/Medical College of Wisconsin.</div>
    
    
  </div> <!-- End of body -->

</article>
  </div>
  <div class="views-row-even">

  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://traffic.libsyn.com/secure/pnas-science-sessions-podcast/amandaRodewaldIvanRudikCatherineKlingPodcast.mp3" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Hazards of ozone pollution to birds</a></div>
